SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0   60 Hobbes asserted in seventeenth-century England that men were consumed by a violent hypermasculinity that was problematic to even basic efforts at societal peace. n29 Note that Hobbes was indicting men as a sex , not " men " in the universalist sense that subsumes women. n30 Men , on his account , obsessively devoted themselves to the protection of their honor , and even the mildest social slights would set them off. n31 Worse , Hobbes believed that for men violence was not simply a means to an end , but that men actually relished opportunities to inflict it and did not flinch from those moments when they had to endure it for the pacific and disciplined business of constitutional democracy. n32 For Hobbes , the only type of government suitable for men was an absolute monarchy that was strong enough to clamp down on their hypermasculine passions. SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0   63 " n36 Further , " conflicts were rapidly translated into the language of the sword , " and this was especially so when they concerned politics or religion , honor. n37 Often , the only method of expression for dissidence appeared to be violent threats , as if the social demands of tolerating another 's competing opinion weighed unbearably on one 's honor. n38 In this atmosphere , the gentry fought to get the best pew in the church , n39 nobility dueled over who would get the most honored seats in court , n40 squires clashed over election as knights and for membership on commissions , n41 and nobility fought for the prestige of the monarch 's attention. n42 According to Professor Stone , " in a society that was even more obsessed with status than with money , intangibles of this sort aroused passions which often could only be appeased in blood .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0   78 " ( n66 ) A fight ensued and witnesses reported that all of Durant 's " face was beblodied. " ( n67 ) Also consider an episode from 1604 between a group of Cambridge " gentlemen Ward protested that Samuel Woodley , while deputy proctor at the university , had no right to confiscate their rapiers and daggers. ( n69 ) Feeling slighted , Garth and Ward told the townspeople that Woodley " was but some cowardly fellow not the mann that he was reported or taken to be , " and also called Woodley 's brother a coward. ( n70 ) A fight ensued , and Garth greeted Samuel with a dagger , warning , " Gods wounds keepe backe or I will let out yor gutts. " ( n71 ) In another instance , a group of " gentlemen scholars " were indignant that a stable boy had carelessly blocked their path with his horse. ( n72 ) The scholars , in an act that would bid defiance to the modern stereotype of the shy and gentle academic , " boxed " the boy 's ears and beat the horse. ( n73 ) The boy resented his ill-treatment and threw a bone at the gentlemen scholars as he scurried away. ( n74 ) One of the scholars repaid the boy 's insolence by stabbing complained that some scholars , feeling that the innkeeper had insulted their honor , had " misused injured the innkeeper by pulling him by the beard kicking offering to strike upp his heles. " ( n75 ) <p> Hypermasculine violence was not limited to nobles or scholars .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0   79 William Maphew and John Trott , two Cambridge cordwainers , came to blows after the former showed off his boots to his friend at an alehouse. ( n76 ) Trott found the act impudent and threw one of the boots to the ground , thereby causing Maphew to say that the boot was " as good worke as you make , " and then a fight ensued. ( n77 ) After the cordwainer John Dod called him a liar , the gentleman Henry Beston reminded the former that " he Beston did come of a better stock kynn , then Dod or any of his kynn did , " and , for punctuation , slapped him on the face. ( n78 ) These were hardly isolated incidents as " nearly one-third of the assault cases heard by the Cambridge justified violent responses as understandable if not appropriate reactions .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  108 Patriarchalism came to institutional realization in England under King James I in the late sixteenth century. ( n98 ) Contra Hobbes 's impersonal diction of authorization , King James gave us the idiom of familial affect : <p> A good King , thinking his highest honour to consist in the due discharge of his calling , emploieth all his studie and paines , to procure and maintaine , by the making and execution of good Lawes , the well-fare and peace of his people ; and as their naturall father and kindly Master , thinketh his greatest contentment standeth in their prosperitie , and his greatest suretie in hauing their hearts , subiecting his owne priuate affections and appetites to the weale and standing of his Subiects. ( n99 ) <p> King James echoed these views elsewhere : " As the Father by his fatherly duty is bound to care for children ; even so is the king bound to care for all his subjects .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  109 " ( n100 ) <p> Yet if the king was morally expected to care for his subjects , the subjects owed him unconditional obedience : <p> If the children may upon any pretext that can be imagined , lawfully rise up against their Father , cut him off , choose any other whom they please in his roome ; and if the body for the weale of it , may for any infirmitie that can be in the head , strike it off , then I can not deny that the people may rebell , controll , and displace , or cut off their king at their owne pleasure , and upon respects moving them. ( n101 ) <p> Overthrowing a king , a justifiable act from our present perspective , is made unthinkable by equating it with the taboo of patricide .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  111 " ( n102 ) According to patriarchalism , if any legal limits were to be set on the king , they were to be , like the social limits on the father , entirely self-imposed. ( n103 ) " Patriarchalism , at its base , treated status as natural and supported authority and duty without reciprocity. " ( n104 ) Patriarchalism thus denies masculinity 's constituent properties in power , strength , and independence to everyone save the king ; as figurative children , male subjects are infantilized and their manhood preempted .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  167 " ( n144 ) This situation imperils person and property. ( n145 ) What began as a placid state of nature degenerates into a state of war where men seek to subdue each other. ( n146 ) To leave this state of war , Locke argued , men must consent with each other civil society that men can establish indifferent judges with powers of enforcement. ( n147 ) According to Locke , civil society is formed when men come together and agree to abstain from exercising their individual natural rights to enforce the law of nature. SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  297 <p> They developed it to include the democratic attitude of treating each other with civility and even affection. ( n254 ) By doing so , they adopted an ethos that tended to evince those social habits conducive to political cooperation among men of differing views and unequal stations. ( n255 ) Part of this ethos derived from the Enlightenment philosophy that was spreading over Western Europe at this time. ( n256 ) As its name implies , the Enlightenment sought to " enlighten " men 's minds by ridding them of superstitions and stifling traditions. ( n257 ) Yet the Enlightenment was not limited to the cultivation of men 's reason ; it also sought to help men become civil and kind by eliminating the barbarism and cruelty of monarchic culture. ( n258 ) <p> A chief illustration was available in the new outlook toward childrearing .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  304 No less important , Locke implied that parental authority could not extend to govern children in the political realm , because parental authority did not equate to political authority. ( n260 ) Elsewhere he advised parents that <p> in a great many things the child must be trusted to his own conduct , since there can not always be a guard upon him , except what you have put into his own mind by good principles and established habits , which is the best and surest , and therefore most to be taken care of. ( n261 ) <p> Such views were widespread by the eighteenth century. ( n262 ) Instead of coercion , parents were expected to treat their children with love and to prepare them to lead independent lives as adults. ( n263 ) <p> If parents were supposed to raise their children in preparation for republican government , men were already expected to exemplify those virtues necessary for its success .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  325 " ( n277 ) In a show of political inclusion , he eagerly informed the congregations that the " affectionate expressions of your address again excite my gratitude , and receive my warmest acknowledgements understanding of Washington by turning to an analysis of Washington 's favorite play , Joseph Addison 's Cato. ( n279 ) Although penned by an Englishman , it became the chief artistic voice for the Americans ' republicanism. ( n280 ) Washington saw Cato many times and often quoted from it. ( n281 ) He also had it staged for dispirited troops at Valley Forge , and , when his officers threatened mutiny in 1783 , he shamed them , as only he could , by reciting apt lines from the play. ( n282 ) The play celebrates public-spiritedness in the service of war , and so one can understand why it would resonate with Americans during the Revolution. ( n283 ) Much of the dialogue , however , lauds those virtues which are peculiarly useful for peace and cooperation .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  345 Evoking the older meaning of civility as citizenship , Washington bypassed a potential opportunity for emperorship to become a regular citizen. ( n291 ) This performance , of course , was consciously staged and Washington " knew at once that he had acquired instant fame as a modern Cincinnatus , " making him internationally famous. ( n292 ) Still , what is important here is that Washington 's public civility was a silent admission of his own democratic dependence on the good wishes of the people. ( n293 ) In his grand coyness , Washington acknowledged that a leader , even one who was venerated , ran a risk in openly seeking political power in a republican nation ; the presidency could not be seized by one but had to be offered by the many. ( n294 ) The young republic had come a long way from rule by a king who was , in Paine 's words , the " principal ruffian of some restless gang .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  348 The Third and Fourth Amendments of the Constitution contain , respectively , the right against the quartering of troops in private homes(n296) and the right against unreasonable searches and seizures of persons , houses , papers , and effects. ( n297 ) One can understand both Amendments as prohibiting governmental intrusion because it is violative of one 's dignity , and thus , by convention , rude ; imagine the uncivil handling one would experience by a throng of soldiers who have their way in one 's home(n298) or by even something as commonplace as a pat-down by the constable. ( n299 ) Perhaps most conspicuously , the Constitution does not permit cruel and unusual punishment , ( n300 ) the grossest example of brutish intemperance by the government .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  349 Here , one will remember civility 's philology as citizenship and the right to equal dignity that imbues it. ( n301 ) What makes cruel and unusual punishment uncivil is not only its outward barbarity but also its attack on the principle of equal dignity. ( n302 ) latter view of civility. ( n303 ) The Religion Clauses prohibit majority religions that control government from degrading the dignity of minority faiths. ( n304 ) We can infer that Article VI performs the same function by banning the government from administering religious tests as a precondition to hold office. ( n305 ) The First Amendment 's rights of speech and press protect people and publishers against an analogous degradation for their political and social beliefs. ( n306 ) <p> 3 .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  439 " ( n374 ) Courts have read the clause in its " dormant " form to prevent a state from discriminating against others to benefit financially its own residents. ( n375 ) For instance , under the Dormant Commerce Clause , Maine would have to show that there are good public-regarding reasons rooted in health and safety , rather than economic protectionism , for forbidding the introduction of live baitfish from other states. ( n376 ) The same logic applies to the Privileges and Immunities Clause of Article IV. THE AMBIVALENT PLACE OF THE GENTLEMAN IN THE CONSTITUTIONAL ORDER From the perspective of constitutional enterprise , should we create and sustain conditions for the ideal of the gentleman to thrive ?

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0  443 As construed by the Supreme Court , the First Amendment protects speech that can be terribly offensive : racist speech , ( n380 ) subversive speech , ( n381 ) pornography that glorifies rape , ( n382 ) and bawdy profanity. ( n383 ) All of this speech is protected partly because of its potential to facilitate the audience in discovering an idea of truth. ( n384 ) The premise here is that the audience is more likely to ascertain the truth if it has access to a diversity of ideas and viewpoints. ( n385 ) A proper man , as general merits of this premise. ( n386 ) Madison and Adams , for example , had implored men to keep an open mind as they commanded themselves in unending deliberation. ( n387 ) By contrast , such deliberation was incompatible with both the zealous myopia which plagued Hobbes 's hypermasculine men and the reflexive deference to social betters which infantilized Filmer 's subjects. ( n388 ) <p> Then there is the role of the gentleman 's civility .

SENTPOS COCA_acad_wlp_2009_04_FILE##4071376 COCA  A    0 1487 James Wilson , one of the six original Justices of the U.S. Supreme Court , also captured the ethos of the times in these remarks from the mid eighteenth century : <p> The wisest and most benign constitution of a rational and moral system is that , in which the degree of private affection , most useful to the individual , is , at the same time , consistent with the greatest interest of the system ; and in which the degree of social affection , most useful to the system , is , at the same time , productive of the greatest happiness to the individual .
